# Historical Patterns

## A Gentle Inquiry into the Fusion of Power and Apocalyptic Purpose in Western Expansion

### A Crossroads of Disciplines: Mapping the Fields This Paper Engages

Before presenting the revised framework, we must situate this
investigation within the academic landscape it traverses. Like the
previous papers, this one does not belong to a single discipline but
stands at the intersection of multiple fields. Its central contribution
is not the discovery of new historical facts but the articulation of a
**pattern-based lens** for re-examining familiar history---a lens that
reveals repeating structures across scales and centuries.

#### 1. The Macro-Historical Framework: Jiang Xueqin and Structural Pattern Analysis

The paper's methodological orientation finds a significant parallel in
the work of Sino-Canadian historian and geopolitical theorist Jiang
Xueqin. Jiang, a Yale-educated scholar based in Beijing, has developed a
framework he calls "Predictive History" through his popular YouTube
channel of the same name. His approach explicitly applies "structural
historical analysis, game theory, and concepts inspired by Isaac
Asimov's fictional psychohistory" to interpret and predict major
geopolitical developments.

Jiang's method rests on a fundamental premise that aligns with this
paper's orientation: **recurring historical patterns, when properly
understood, reveal the underlying structures that drive human events
across different eras and contexts**. He argues that "both historical
patterns and game theory can be used to accurately predict future
events" because the same structural dynamics tend to produce similar
outcomes under similar conditions.

What makes Jiang's framework particularly relevant to this inquiry is
his attention to the deep, macro-historical forces that shape
civilizations. His lecture series "Civilization" traces "the narrative
and ideological evolution of human societies from prehistory to the
contemporary period, with particular emphasis on the unifying and
mobilizing power of transcendent religious and civilizational belief
systems". This focus on how belief systems mobilize populations aligns
directly with this paper's examination of apocalyptic and
quasi-religious narratives as drivers of expansion.

Jiang's analysis of contemporary geopolitics draws heavily on historical
parallels. In his widely discussed 2024 lecture, he compared a potential
US-Iran conflict to the Athenian Sicilian Expedition during the
Peloponnesian War---"a massive, ambitious military campaign launched by
Athens that ultimately ended in total catastrophe". This methodological
move---using deep historical patterns to illuminate present
dynamics---exemplifies the pattern-based approach this paper advocates.

**This paper draws on Jiang's framework** to situate its own
pattern-based analysis within a broader scholarly conversation about
macro-historical structures. Jiang's work demonstrates that pattern
recognition, when done rigorously, can generate testable hypotheses
about geopolitical outcomes---two of his three major 2024 predictions
(Trump's return to power and a US-Iran conflict) have already been borne
out.

#### 2. The Steppe Hypothesis: Geographical Migration as Pattern Generator

Your extension of the framework---that "the European pattern was shaped
by a continuous stream of steppes people invading Europe, based on
geographical migration"---engages a substantial body of scholarship on
the role of Eurasian steppe nomads in shaping European civilization.
This is a macro-historical pattern operating at an even deeper temporal
scale than the Crusades-to-present arc this paper primarily examines.

The geographical determinism implicit in this hypothesis finds
theoretical grounding in Jiang's work, which acknowledges "geographical
determinism" as one of his analytical frameworks. The argument would be
that Europe's historical trajectory cannot be understood without
accounting for the repeated influx of steppe peoples---Scythians, Huns,
Avars, Magyars, Mongols, and others---whose migrations shaped European
political geography, military technology, and cultural development over
millennia.

This pattern operates at what might be called the **tectonic scale** of
history. Just as geological forces shape the terrain on which human
events unfold, the centuries-long pressure of steppe migrations created
conditions that predisposed European societies toward certain
responses---fortification, militarization, and eventually, outward
expansion as a release valve for internal pressures. The crusading
impulse that this paper identifies as a template for later expansion may
itself be understood as a response, in part, to the deep historical
memory of invasion from the East.

**This paper acknowledges this deeper pattern** while focusing on the
more recent fusion of power and meaning that characterized Western
expansion. The steppe hypothesis reminds us that the patterns we observe
at one scale are often themselves expressions of patterns operating at
larger scales---a recursive insight that Jiang's framework, with its
emphasis on structural analysis across time, is well-equipped to
accommodate.

#### 3. Historiography and the Revisionist Tradition

The paper engages most directly with the revisionist tradition in
American historiography. Scholars have long moved beyond the celebratory
narratives of nineteenth-century historians who "glorified expansionism
as a heroic struggle, a romantic adventure, a benevolent mission, and a
Darwinian right or inevitability". Postcolonial and revisionist
historians have instead "asserted a more critical lens," revealing that
"viewed from the perspective of the conquered and colonized, the United
States has been in a continual imperial mode since its colonial status,
pushing ever westward."

Robert J. Miller's scholarship on the Doctrine of Discovery provides
crucial legal-historical grounding for this revisionist perspective.
Miller demonstrates that **Manifest Destiny arose directly from the
legal principles and elements of the Doctrine of Discovery** established
in fifteenth-century papal bulls. These documents authorized Christian
nations to claim non-Christian lands and subjugate their inhabitants,
creating principles that became Christian international law and were
subsequently adopted into American colonial and state law, incorporated
into the U.S. Constitution, and affirmed by the Supreme Court in
*Johnson v. M'Intosh* (1823).

John Christopher Pinheiro's work on the Mexican-American War further
substantiates the pattern. Pinheiro documents how "many Americans argued
that Roman Catholicism was incompatible with civil and religious
liberty" and pointed to "Mexico's political troubles as an example of
this incompatibility in action." This pre-war framing "helped to
establish a preconception of Mexicans as a cognitively inferior,
superstitious, and lazy race," while evangelical Protestants "saw the
war as a Providential opportunity to evangelize Catholic Mexico with a
'pure' gospel."

**This paper builds upon these historiographical foundations** by
extracting the underlying pattern: the fusion of elite material ambition
with popular apocalyptic narrative. It then asks whether this same
pattern can be observed operating at different scales and in different
contexts---from the Crusades to contemporary geopolitics.

#### 4. The Crusades and the Medieval Origins of Western Expansion

The paper's framing of the Crusades as a "template" finds support in
specialized scholarship on medieval expansion and its afterlife in later
centuries. The crusading ideal proved remarkably adaptable: when
Jerusalem was lost, the framework found new targets---heretics in
southern France, pagans in the Baltic, political opponents of the papacy
in Italy.

This adaptability is crucial for the pattern-based view. The crusade was
not a single event but a **meme complex**---a cluster of ideas (holy
war, remission of sins, cosmic struggle) that could attach itself to
diverse material projects. The same structure that sent knights to
Jerusalem could later send conquistadors to the Americas and settlers
across the continent.

**This paper draws on crusade historiography** not for new archival
discoveries but for the pattern it reveals: a religious framework
flexible enough to legitimate diverse projects of expansion while
providing participants with cosmic meaning.

#### 5. The Frankfurt School and the Critique of Enlightenment

Your bibliography includes Adorno and Horkheimer's *Dialectic of
Enlightenment*, and this philosophical tradition is central to the
paper's deeper argument. The Frankfurt School theorists argued that
Enlightenment reason, intended to liberate humanity from myth, had
itself become a new form of mythology---a tool of domination rather than
liberation.

This framework directly parallels the paper's argument about the fusion
of material interest and cosmic meaning. For Adorno and Horkheimer, the
Enlightenment's promise of freedom through reason was betrayed when
reason became instrumental---a tool for controlling nature and other
humans rather than for understanding. The result was a new form of
irrationalism disguised as rationality.

**This paper extends this critique** by showing how the fusion operates
not only in Enlightenment philosophy but in the concrete historical
projects of Western expansion. The "dialectic of enlightenment" is not
an abstract philosophical problem but a lived historical pattern visible
in the Crusades, colonialism, Manifest Destiny, and modern
interventions.

#### 6. Political Psychology and the Contagion of Leadership

Your extension of the framework---that a psychopathic leader will induce
psychopathic values and behaviors in a population---engages a growing
literature in political psychology and social contagion theory. This is
where the pattern-based view becomes truly powerful: it suggests that
the same dynamics that operate at the scale of centuries also operate at
the scale of decades, years, and even individual lives.

The literature on authoritarianism has long documented how populations
can internalize the values of authoritarian leaders. Theodor Adorno's
*The Authoritarian Personality* (1950) was an early attempt to
understand how psychological dispositions make populations susceptible
to fascist appeals. More recent work has examined how leaders shape not
only policies but the moral and emotional climate of entire societies.

The pattern-based view suggests that **Nazism is better explained
through this lens than through exceptionalist accounts** that treat it
as a unique historical aberration. The fusion of elite ambition
(industrialists, military officers, party officials) with popular
apocalyptic narrative (the myth of Aryan destiny, the cosmic struggle
against Bolshevism and international Jewry) follows precisely the same
structure as the Crusades, colonialism, and Manifest Destiny.

This is not to minimize the specific horrors of the Holocaust, nor to
suggest that all expansions are morally equivalent. It is to recognize
that the underlying pattern---the engine of power fused with
meaning---operates across contexts. When a psychopathic leader ascends
to power, they do not merely impose their pathology on a passive
population. They activate existing cultural narratives, channel material
anxieties, and provide a framework that makes psychopathic behavior seem
not only acceptable but righteous.

#### 7. Network Theory and Pattern Recognition Across Scales

The pattern-based view finds methodological support in contemporary
network theory and complexity science. Recent work on information
cascades and social contagion examines how beliefs and behaviors
propagate through populations, and how the structure of social networks
shapes what spreads.

The key insight for our purposes is that **patterns can repeat across
scales without requiring direct historical causation**. The Crusades did
not cause Manifest Destiny. But the same underlying dynamics---elite
ambition seeking legitimation, populations seeking meaning, the fusion
of the two into an engine of expansion---can operate independently in
different times and places because they reflect fundamental features of
human social organization.

This is what the paper means by "patterns who can repeat and appear at
many scales." It is not claiming that later expansions were consciously
modeled on earlier ones, though sometimes they were. It is claiming that
the same structural dynamics tend to produce similar outcomes across
different historical contexts.

**This paper draws on network theory** not for mathematical modeling but
for its conceptual vocabulary: cascades, contagion, scale invariance,
emergent behavior. These concepts provide a language for describing how
patterns propagate and repeat without requiring conspiracy or direct
influence.

#### 8. The Taboo Zone: Pattern Recognition as Suspect

We must acknowledge, as with the previous papers, the academic taboos
this work challenges. Pattern-based historical analysis occupies an
uncomfortable position in the academy. It risks being accused of:

- **Reductionism:** collapsing complex historical events into a single
  explanatory framework.
- **Conspiracy theory:** suggesting hidden connections across time and
  space.
- **Presentism:** reading contemporary concerns into the past.
- **Moral equivalency:** comparing events that are not morally
  comparable.

These risks are real. The paper must navigate them carefully. But the
taboo against pattern recognition also has costs. It can prevent us from
seeing genuine structural continuities that operate across centuries. It
can trap us in exceptionalist narratives that treat each historical
event as sui generis, obscuring the dynamics they share.

Jiang Xueqin's work has faced similar critiques. Some commentators have
questioned whether his predictions "rely on selective historical
analogies, speculative game-theoretic reasoning, and untestable
assumptions". Yet the accuracy of two of his three major 2024
predictions has forced a reconsideration of his methodology. The
pattern-based approach, when applied rigorously, can generate testable
claims about the future---a powerful argument for its validity.

**This paper accepts these risks** because the patterns it identifies
are too persistent and too consequential to ignore. The fusion of elite
ambition and popular apocalyptic narrative has shaped Western expansion
for a millennium. To refuse to see it because seeing it is uncomfortable
is to remain willfully blind.

### Introduction: An Invitation to Look Anew

Every society tells itself a story about its past. These stories are not
false, but they are inevitably partial. They emphasize certain events
and downplay others; they highlight noble intentions and sometimes
obscure inconvenient consequences. This is not malice. It is how
collective memory functions. It provides cohesion, meaning, and a sense
of moral direction.

This paper is an invitation to consider an additional layer to the
Western story. It is not an attempt to replace the existing narrative,
but to supplement it with a lens that reveals certain recurring
patterns---patterns that connect the Crusader marching toward Jerusalem,
the Conquistador sailing for the New World, the pioneer venturing west
under Manifest Destiny, the Nazi marching under the swastika, and the
modern soldier deployed in the name of spreading freedom.

The lens is this: **Western expansion has been consistently driven by a
fusion between the material ambitions of powerful groups and the
apocalyptic or quasi-religious yearnings of broader populations.** These
two forces, the cynical and the sincere, have repeatedly converged to
create an engine of enormous power---one capable of justifying immense
violence, sustaining itself across centuries, and adapting its language
to suit each new era.

To examine this is not to accuse every participant of bad faith. On the
contrary, it is to take sincere belief seriously, and to ask the
difficult question: how does sincere belief become entangled with
systems of power that it would otherwise abhor? And what might it mean
to recognize this entanglement?

### Part I: The Engine Defined

Before tracing the engine through history, we must understand its
components.

**The First Component: Pragmatic Ambition**

Every society has its elites---individuals and groups who accumulate
disproportionate influence over resources, institutions, and
decision-making. Their motivations are generally not mysterious. They
seek to preserve and expand their position. This can mean securing
access to valuable trade routes, acquiring land and resources,
establishing military advantage, or controlling markets. In the medieval
period, this meant popes seeking authority over kings and Italian
city-states seeking control of Mediterranean trade. In the colonial era,
it meant monarchs filling treasuries and companies seeking sugar and
cotton. In the modern era, it means securing oil, maintaining global
military basing, and ensuring favorable conditions for multinational
corporations.

This component is not inherently evil. It is a description of how power
tends to operate. But it is incomplete without the second component.

**The Second Component: The Search for Cosmic Meaning**

Human beings are not merely economic actors. We are meaning-makers. We
crave narratives that place our lives within a larger story, that give
our struggles purpose and our sacrifices significance. The most powerful
of these narratives have often been religious, but they can also take
secularized forms: the mission to spread democracy, the duty to defend
civilization, the destiny to tame a wilderness, the destiny of a master
race.

When these two components operate separately, their effects are limited.
Ambitious elites without a compelling narrative can only rule through
brute force, which is expensive and unstable. A population with powerful
beliefs but no organized power can sustain a movement, but may struggle
to shape large-scale events. The danger---and the engine---emerges when
the two fuse.

**The Fusion**

When elite ambition aligns with popular apocalyptic or quasi-religious
narrative, each amplifies the other. The elite gain legitimacy, popular
enthusiasm, and a motivated workforce or soldiery. The population gains
a sense of cosmic purpose, a conviction that their actions serve not
just material interests but divine or moral destiny. The fusion produces
a force capable of immense destruction, precisely because it combines
the cold calculation of power with the white-hot energy of faith.

Crucially, this fusion does not require conscious conspiracy. It often
emerges organically, as elites learn to speak the language of the
people, and as religious leaders interpret events in ways that align
with the interests of the powerful. The result is a system that
perpetuates itself without anyone needing to be fully aware of its
mechanics.

**The Deep Temporal Context: Steppe Invasions and European Formation**

To understand why European societies developed this particular engine of
expansion, we must look deeper---to the millennia of steppe invasions
that shaped the continent's genetic, cultural, and political landscape.
From the Scythians in the first millennium BCE to the Huns in the fourth
and fifth centuries, the Avars and Magyars in subsequent centuries, and
the Mongol invasions of the thirteenth century, Europe was repeatedly
subjected to waves of mounted warriors from the Eurasian steppe.

This continuous pressure had several lasting effects:

- **Militarization:** European societies developed fortified structures
  and military technologies (the castle, heavy cavalry, eventually
  gunpowder) partially in response to steppe threats.
- **Cultural memory:** The figure of the "barbarian" from the East
  became embedded in European consciousness, later to be projected onto
  other peoples during expansion.
- **Release valve dynamics:** When internal pressures built up---surplus
  warrior populations, religious fervor, economic ambition---the memory
  of invasion from the East could be inverted into a mandate for
  invasion toward the East (the Crusades) or, when the Atlantic opened,
  toward the West.

The steppe hypothesis suggests that the engine this paper describes did
not emerge from a vacuum. It was forged in response to millennia of
pressure from the East---a pressure that shaped European societies in
ways that predisposed them toward expansion when conditions allowed.

### Part II: The Prototype---The Crusades as Template

The Crusades provide our clearest view of this engine in its formative
stage.

Consider the situation in late 11th-century Europe. The papal reform
movement had strengthened the papacy, but it still struggled for
authority against secular rulers and the Eastern Church. The feudal
system produced a class of younger sons of nobles with no land and no
prospects. Italian maritime republics like Venice and Genoa sought
advantage in Mediterranean trade. Into this environment came Pope Urban
II's call at Clermont in 1095.

The stated purpose was religious: to liberate Jerusalem from Muslim rule
and to aid Eastern Christians. The promise was apocalyptic: participants
would receive remission of sins, a spiritual reward that transcended
earthly concerns. The enemy was framed as cosmic: not merely political
opponents, but defilers of holy places, enemies of God.

Yet the material outcomes were unmistakable. The Crusades extended papal
influence. They provided an outlet for restless warriors, channeling
their violence outward rather than inward. They opened trade routes that
enriched European merchants. They established Crusader states that
functioned as new feudal domains for ambitious nobles.

Were the Crusaders merely cynical adventurers using religion as a cover?
Some may have been. But the historical record suggests that most
participants were sincere in their faith. They believed they were
undertaking a holy mission. The tragedy---and the pattern---is that
their sincere belief served interests far beyond their own salvation.

The Crusades also reveal the engine's adaptability. When Jerusalem was
lost, the framework did not collapse. It simply found new targets:
heretics in southern France, pagans in the Baltic, political opponents
of the papacy in Italy. The crusading ideal became a flexible meme,
capable of legitimating diverse projects of expansion and domination.

### Part III: The Expansion---Colonialism and the New World

When European powers turned their attention across the Atlantic, the
crusading framework traveled with them, though its language evolved.

The material ambitions are well-documented. Spain sought gold and
silver. Portugal sought a route to the spice trade. England and France
sought territory and resources. Joint-stock companies emerged to finance
and profit from colonial ventures. These were not hidden motives; they
were openly declared.

Yet the enterprise could not have succeeded on greed alone. It required
a legitimating narrative. This came from the same fusion we have
observed.

The Doctrine of Discovery, articulated in papal bulls of the 15th
century---Pope Nicholas V's *Romanus Pontifex* (1455) and Pope Alexander
VI's *Inter Caetera* (1493)---provided the theological framework:
Christian nations had the right to claim lands not ruled by Christians,
and to subjugate their inhabitants for the purpose of conversion. These
documents established what became Christian international law, creating
principles that would shape European colonization for centuries.

The Puritan colonies of New England offer a particularly pure expression
of the fusion. John Winthrop's vision of a "City upon a Hill" explicitly
invoked biblical imagery. The colonists saw themselves as a new Israel,
fleeing bondage in Europe to establish a holy commonwealth in the
wilderness. Their expansion was not conquest but fulfillment of
covenant. The native inhabitants were not nations with their own rights
and histories, but either potential converts or demonic impediments to
be cleared away.

The same framework justified the enslavement of Africans. A twisted
theology developed: Africans were descendants of Ham, cursed to
servitude; their enslavement, followed by forced conversion, was
presented as rescuing them from paganism. The material interests of
plantation owners and slave traders fused with a narrative that gave
cosmic meaning to atrocity.

### Part IV: The Culmination---Manifest Destiny and the Nineteenth Century

The 19th century gave this fusion its most explicit American expression:
Manifest Destiny.

The phrase itself, coined in 1845, captured both components perfectly.
"Manifest" suggested obviousness, self-evidence, truth that required no
argument. "Destiny" suggested inevitability, divine ordination, a future
already written. The expansion of the United States across the continent
was framed not as conquest but as fulfillment.

**The Legal Foundation:** As Robert J. Miller's scholarship
demonstrates, Manifest Destiny arose directly from the legal principles
of the Doctrine of Discovery. This international law was adopted into
American colonial and state law, incorporated into the U.S.
Constitution, and affirmed by the Supreme Court in *Johnson v. M'Intosh*
(1823). The framework was not merely rhetorical; it was embedded in
American law.

**The Religious Dimension:** John Christopher Pinheiro's research on the
Mexican-American War reveals how anti-Catholicism and crusading language
shaped American perceptions. Many Americans argued that "Roman
Catholicism was incompatible with civil and religious liberty" and
pointed to "Mexico's political troubles as an example of this
incompatibility in action." Evangelical Protestants "saw the war as a
Providential opportunity to evangelize Catholic Mexico with a 'pure'
gospel."

The cost to indigenous peoples was catastrophic. Yet it was framed
within the same narrative. Native Americans were either obstacles to
progress or people to be "civilized." The Indian Removal Act was
presented as humanitarian---separating native peoples from the
corrupting influence of white settlement. The Trail of Tears was
justified as necessary for the greater good.

### Part V: The Pathology---Nazism as Pattern

The pattern-based view finds one of its clearest expressions in Nazi
Germany. Here, the fusion of elite ambition and popular apocalyptic
narrative operated with terrifying efficiency.

**The Material Component:** German industrialists, military officers,
and party officials had concrete material interests: rearmament,
territorial expansion, access to resources, elimination of political
opposition. The SS became an economic empire, operating businesses and
exploiting slave labor. These were not irrational actors pursuing
abstract ideology; they were rational actors pursuing power and profit.

**The Narrative Component:** The Nazi movement offered a cosmic
narrative: the Aryan race as the bearer of civilization, locked in an
apocalyptic struggle with Jewish Bolshevism, destined to expand into
living space in the East. This was not merely propaganda for mass
consumption. Leading Nazis sincerely believed elements of this
mythology. Himmler's SS was organized as a quasi-religious order,
complete with rituals, ceremonies, and an eschatological vision.

**The Fusion:** Elite interests and popular belief amplified each other.
Industrialists profited from rearmament while believing they were
serving the nation. Soldiers marched east convinced they were defending
civilization against subhuman enemies. Ordinary Germans participated in
atrocities while believing they were fulfilling their duty to a higher
cause.

**The Psychopathic Leader Hypothesis:** Adolf Hitler exhibited classic
psychopathic traits: lack of empathy, grandiosity, manipulativeness,
pathological lying. But the horror of Nazism cannot be explained by
Hitler's psychology alone. The fusion occurred because Hitler's
pathology resonated with existing cultural narratives (German
nationalism, antisemitism, anti-communism) and material conditions
(economic crisis, resentment of Versailles). The population did not
become clinically psychopathic, but they adopted psychopathic
values---the devaluation of outgroups, the suspension of moral
constraints, the willingness to commit atrocities.

This is the pattern at work. Nazism was not a unique historical
aberration. It was an expression of the same engine that drove the
Crusades, colonialism, and Manifest Destiny---adapted to German cultural
materials and twentieth-century conditions.

### Part VI: The Adaptation---The Modern Era

After World War II, explicit religious language became less acceptable
in formal statecraft. Yet the underlying structure of the fusion
adapted, finding new language for old patterns.

The Cold War provided the first great modern framework. The enemy was
communism, framed not merely as a political opponent but as an "evil
empire," an atheistic force threatening civilization itself. The
language was secularized---"freedom" and "democracy" replaced
"Christendom" and "the faith"---but the structure was identical. The
West had a cosmic mission: to defend the free world, to contain evil, to
ultimately triumph in a struggle of light against darkness.

The material interests were substantial. The military-industrial complex
grew enormously. Defense contracts flowed to corporations. Strategic
bases were established around the world. Access to oil in the Middle
East became a paramount concern.

The War on Terror, launched after September 11, 2001, represents the
purest modern expression of the fusion. The enemy was not a state but an
abstract evil: "terror" itself. This framing allowed for endless war, as
terror could never be fully defeated. It justified interventions across
the globe, suspension of civil liberties at home, and a permanent state
of emergency.

The language was explicitly Manichaean. President George W. Bush spoke
of an "axis of evil." He framed the conflict as a struggle between good
and evil, between civilization and barbarism. "Either you are with us,
or you are with the terrorists." This was the language of crusade,
updated for a secular age.

Yet the material outcomes were concrete. The Iraq War, justified through
claims about weapons of mass destruction that proved false, secured
American military presence in an oil-rich region and awarded lucrative
contracts to defense and reconstruction firms.

### Part VII: The Contemporary Edge---Israel and Eschatology

Nowhere is this fusion more visible today than in the intersection of
American foreign policy toward Israel and the eschatological beliefs of
some Christian fundamentalists.

The material and strategic interests are clear. Israel has long been
viewed by American strategists as a crucial ally in a volatile region.
It provides intelligence cooperation, military basing access, and a
counterweight to hostile regional powers.

Yet this alliance also receives fervent support from a different
quarter: evangelical Christians who believe that the gathering of Jews
in the Holy Land is a necessary precondition for the End Times and the
Second Coming of Christ. For these believers, support for Israel is not
a matter of geopolitics but of prophecy. It is a sacred duty.

Recent scholarship documents this fusion. Motti Inbari and Kirill
Bumin's research on Christian Zionism finds "significant theological and
eschatological differences among evangelical pastors which are likely to
have direct implications on their current foreign policy views, and
especially on their support for the State of Israel." Stephen Sizer's
work identifies "seven basic theological tenets that distinguish the
various strands within the Christian Zionist movement," including
beliefs that have direct "political consequences."

The convergence is striking. Strategists and believers find themselves
advocating for the same policies, for entirely different reasons. Each
reinforces the other. The believers provide grassroots energy and voting
power. The strategists provide access and influence. The result is a
policy that can survive even when it conflicts with other American
interests or values.

### Part VIII: Seeing Gently---Compassion and Complexity

At this point, a reader might feel uncomfortable. The framework can seem
to reduce history to a sordid tale of manipulation and delusion. It can
feel like an attack on sincere faith, on genuine patriotism, on the
noble intentions of countless individuals who have sought to do good.

This discomfort must be taken seriously. If the lens cannot accommodate
compassion, it is not a lens but a weapon.

So let us be clear about what this framework does *not* claim.

It does not claim that every participant in these historical processes
was cynical or malevolent. Most were not. The Crusader who marched east,
the colonist who crossed the Atlantic, the pioneer who ventured west,
the German soldier who fought for the Reich, the American soldier who
served in Iraq---these were ordinary human beings, seeking meaning,
security, and purpose. They loved their families, mourned their dead,
and tried, in their own way, to live good lives.

It does not claim that religious or moral belief is merely a mask for
power. Belief is real. It shapes action. It gives life meaning. To
suggest otherwise is to misunderstand what it means to be human.

It does not claim that all outcomes of Western expansion have been
negative. The tradition that produced the Crusades also produced
universities, hospitals, and works of art. The civilization that engaged
in colonialism also developed concepts of human rights, democracy, and
universal dignity. We are not required to choose between celebration and
condemnation.

What the framework does claim is this: **Sincere belief and material
power have repeatedly become entangled in ways that serve the interests
of the powerful while providing cosmic meaning to the population, and
this entanglement has produced consequences that neither group fully
intended or fully perceived.**

The tragedy is not that people believe. The tragedy is that their belief
can be woven into systems of violence and exploitation without their
knowledge or consent.

### Part IX: The Gentle Purpose---Seeing as Liberation

If this framework is not about blame, what is its purpose? Why offer
this lens at all?

The answer is that **seeing clearly is itself a form of liberation.**
Systems of power maintain themselves partly by becoming invisible. They
become the water in which we swim, the air we breathe. We do not
question them because we do not see them. The fusion of elite interest
and popular narrative is most powerful when it is most hidden.

To see the pattern is to begin to question. Not to reject everything,
but to ask: In what ways might my sincere beliefs be serving purposes I
have not considered? In what ways might the narratives I absorb from my
culture be channeling my energy toward ends I would not endorse if I saw
them clearly? In what ways might I be participating in systems that I
would oppose if I understood them?

These are not comfortable questions. But they are the questions of
maturity. A child accepts the stories they are told. An adult learns to
examine them. A society that cannot examine its own stories is a society
that cannot grow.

The gentle purpose of this lens is to invite that examination. Not to
force it, not to shame those who decline it, but to offer it as a tool
for those who seek greater clarity.

Jiang Xueqin's work demonstrates that this kind of seeing has practical
value. By recognizing patterns, we can anticipate outcomes. His
framework, which combines "recurring historical patterns, geopolitical
incentives, and game theory," has generated predictions that have proven
remarkably accurate. This is not prophecy; it is structural analysis
applied rigorously.

### Conclusion: The Continuous Crusade and the Possibility of Awakening

We have traced a pattern across a millennium. From the Crusader marching
to Jerusalem, to the Conquistador sailing for the New World, to the
Puritan building a city on a hill, to the pioneer fulfilling Manifest
Destiny, to the Nazi marching under the swastika, to the modern soldier
fighting to spread freedom, to the believer supporting Israel to hasten
the End Times---through all these, we have seen the same engine at work.

It is the engine of power fused with meaning, of elite ambition
channeling popular faith, of sincere belief serving purposes beyond
itself. It is the continuous crusade.

The pattern-based view reveals that these are not isolated phenomena.
They are expressions of a single, adaptive structure---one that operates
across centuries, across cultures, across scales. The same dynamics that
drove the Crusades drove Manifest Destiny and Nazism and the War on
Terror. Not because later actors consciously imitated earlier ones, but
because the underlying structure of power and meaning tends to produce
similar outcomes in similar conditions.

The deep temporal context of steppe invasions reminds us that even this
engine has preconditions---historical forces that shaped European
societies in ways that made them expansion-prone. The pattern at one
scale is nested within patterns at larger scales.

To name it is not to end it. Patterns this deep do not dissolve simply
because they are recognized. But recognition is a beginning. It is the
moment when we stop being merely carried by the current and begin to see
the current itself. It is the moment when we can ask, for the first
time: Do I want to go where this current is taking me? Is this really
the story I want to be part of?

These questions are not comfortable. They are not easy. But they are the
questions of moral awakening. And if the continuous crusade has taught
us anything, it is that such awakening is always possible---and always,
for those who benefit from the current, deeply threatening.

The purpose of this gentle inquiry has been to make that awakening a
little more possible, a little less frightening, and a little more
available to all of us who swim in waters we did not create and do not
fully see.

### An Invitation to Collaboration

This paper has offered a pattern-based lens for re-examining Western
expansion from the Crusades to the present. It suggests that the same
underlying structure---the fusion of elite ambition with popular
apocalyptic narrative---has operated across centuries and scales,
producing similar outcomes in different contexts.

The framework remains conceptual. It requires testing, refinement, and
application across multiple domains. We invite collaboration from:

- **Historians** to test the framework against archival evidence,
  identifying cases where the fusion operated and cases where it did
  not.
- **Political scientists** to examine how the pattern manifests in
  contemporary geopolitics and foreign policy.
- **Sociologists** to study how apocalyptic narratives spread through
  populations and how they interact with elite interests.
- **Psychologists** to investigate the mechanisms by which populations
  internalize the values of psychopathic leaders.
- **Network theorists** to model how the fusion propagates through
  social systems and how it might be interrupted.
- **Theologians and religious scholars** to bring their understanding of
  apocalyptic traditions to bear on the pattern's religious dimensions.
- **Macro-historians and pattern theorists** working in the tradition of
  Jiang Xueqin to refine our understanding of how deep structural
  patterns shape human events across scales and centuries.

The pattern is real. It matters. It shapes our world in ways we are only
beginning to understand. Understanding it better---with rigor,
compassion, and intellectual humility---is a task worthy of our best
efforts.
